Let me draft this chapter.## Chapter Three
# A Case No One Filed

---

The morning arrived with the enthusiasm of something that had been scheduled to arrive and was determined to meet its deadline regardless of anyone's feelings on the matter.

Robert had not slept.

This was not unusual. Robert had a complicated relationship with sleep — he was theoretically in favor of it, practically opposed to it, and philosophically suspicious of any activity that required him to be unconscious and vulnerable for eight consecutive hours. Sleep was when things happened. Sleep was when letters appeared inside locked doors and filing cabinets opened themselves and the universe made adjustments it didn't want you to see.

He had spent the night with the box.

The photographs showed L.E.E. Unit 142 in various locations around District Ten — a rounded, soft-looking thing that resembled what might happen if a marshmallow achieved sentience and decided to help. In each photo, L.E.E. was with children. In each photo, L.E.E. was doing something gentle: holding a hand, offering what appeared to be a hug, standing patiently while a small human cried into its midsection.

In three of the photographs, the same child appeared.

Small. Dark hair. Eyes that looked like they were seeing things other people couldn't.

The name on the back of each photo: **Bob**.

---

Robert's badge chimed.

This was new. Badges didn't chime. Badges glowed, badges granted access, badges occasionally malfunctioned in ways that made clerks nervous — but they didn't *chime*. Chiming implied communication. Chiming implied that someone, somewhere, wanted Robert's attention.

He pulled out the badge.

The surface displayed text:

**CASE ASSIGNED: L.E.E. UNIT 142**
**STATUS: ACTIVE**
**PRIORITY: ELEVATED**
**ASSIGNED INVESTIGATOR: R.P. MANN**
**NOTE: Please report to District 15 Administrative Office for briefing.**

Robert stared at the badge.

He had not requested a case. He had not filed paperwork. He had not, to his knowledge, done anything except open a box that had been humming at him and listen to a recording that raised more questions than a philosophy exam written by someone who'd recently discovered existentialism.

And yet.

Case assigned.

"Huh," Robert said.

The desk groaned in agreement.

---

District 15 was not a place Robert visited often.

This was deliberate. District 15 was where things went to end — equipment, machinery, the occasional career that had made one too many wrong turns. It was the edge of the dome, the last stop before the wall, the place where the city's unwanted objects went to be dismantled and recycled into things the city wanted again.

It was also, according to his badge, where he needed to be.

Robert put on his coat. The coat was brown, which matched his mood. He checked his pockets: badge, wallet, the metal disc engraved with 142, and a photograph of Bob and L.E.E. that he'd taken from the box without entirely knowing why.

He opened the door.

The rooster was in the hallway.

It was sitting directly in front of his door, as if it had been waiting. Its head was tilted at an angle that suggested deep thought, or possibly judgment. It was hard to tell with roosters.

"Move," Robert said.

The rooster did not move.

"I have a case."

The rooster remained unimpressed by Robert's professional obligations.

"Fine."

Robert stepped over the rooster. The rooster watched him go with the quiet superiority of something that had nowhere to be and all the time in the world to not be there.

---

The transit to District 15 took forty-seven minutes.

Robert knew this because he counted. Counting was something he did when he was thinking, and he was thinking about several things at once: the three R.P. Manns, the recording in the box, the child named Bob, the robot that had disappeared 180 years ago, and the fact that someone had assigned him a case he hadn't asked for to investigate something no one seemed to care about.

The transit car was mostly empty. A woman in a gray coat sat near the front, reading something on a pad. A man in coveralls slept against the window, his breath fogging the glass in rhythmic clouds. And in the back corner, a mop and bucket stood propped against the seat, unattended.

Robert looked at the mop and bucket.

The mop and bucket looked back, inasmuch as cleaning equipment could look at anything.

There was no janitor.

Robert decided not to think about it.

---

District 15 announced itself through smell before it announced itself through sight.

The air changed as the transit car approached the edge — something chemical underneath the regulated atmosphere, something that spoke of metal being separated from metal and purpose being separated from function. The sky was still blue, but it seemed thinner here, like the color had been stretched to cover more area than it was designed for.

The transit station was small. Gray. Functional in the way that things are functional when no one cares enough to make them pleasant.

Robert stepped onto the platform.

A sign welcomed him:

**DISTRICT 15**
**RECYCLING & RECLAMATION**
**"Where Everything Gets Another Chance"**

Someone had added, in marker, beneath the official text: *"Except the things that don't."*

---

The Administrative Office was a squat building near the station, distinguished from the warehouses around it only by the presence of windows and a door that looked like it might actually open if you asked nicely.

Robert asked nicely.

The door opened.

Inside was a waiting room that had given up on the concept of waiting. The chairs were empty. The reception desk was unmanned. A plant in the corner had died so thoroughly that it had begun to fossilize. The only sound was the hum — that hum, the one Robert kept hearing, the one that seemed to live underneath everything now.

"Hello?" Robert called.

His voice echoed.

Nothing answered.

He approached the reception desk. A sign indicated that visitors should take a number. The number dispenser was empty. A second sign, placed next to the first, read: **"If no numbers are available, please wait to be acknowledged."**

Robert waited.

The hum continued.

After approximately three minutes — Robert counted — a door in the back opened.

---

The man who emerged was not what Robert had expected.

He was tall. Neat. Wearing a suit that cost more than Robert's monthly rent and shoes that had probably never touched the recycled ground of District 15. His hair was precise. His posture was precise. Everything about him suggested that he had been assembled from a kit labeled "Management" and had followed the instructions exactly.

"Mr. Mann," the man said.

"That's me."

"I'm aware." He didn't extend a hand. He didn't smile. He looked at Robert the way you might look at a form that had been filled out incorrectly. "I'm Robert Parsifal Mann. District Manager."

Robert's brain did something unusual. It stopped.

"Robert," he said.

"Yes."

"Parsifal."

"Yes."

"Mann."

"Is there an echo in here, or are you having some kind of episode?"

Robert opened his mouth. Closed it. Opened it again.

"We have the same name."

"We have the same *initials*." Parsifal's tone suggested this was a distinction that mattered deeply to him and should matter equally to everyone else. "I'm Robert Parsifal Mann. You're Robert Patrick Mann. Entirely different."

"The same first name."

"A common first name."

"The same last name."

"A common last name."

"The same middle initial."

Parsifal's left eye twitched. It was a small twitch — controlled, professional, the kind of twitch that had been trained not to become anything larger. But Robert saw it.

"Coincidence," Parsifal said. "District Ten has over forty thousand residents. Statistical overlap is inevitable."

"The singing telegram guy said there are three of us."

The twitch became more pronounced.

"The singing telegram," Parsifal said carefully, "is not a reliable source of demographic information."

"He seemed pretty sure."

"He's been trying to deliver a message to 'R.P. Mann' for three days. He's delivered it to me twice. I am not the intended recipient." Parsifal straightened his already straight tie. "The system has a glitch. It will be resolved. That's not why you're here."

"Why am I here?"

"You requested the L.E.E. files."

"I didn't request anything. A filing cabinet opened by itself."

Parsifal stared at him.

Robert stared back.

"A filing cabinet," Parsifal said, "opened by itself."

"Yes."

"And inside was information about a robot that's been missing for one hundred and eighty years."

"Yes."

"And this prompted you to — what? Investigate? A cold case that predates your grandfather?"

"Someone assigned me the case."

"No one assigned you anything."

Robert pulled out his badge. He showed Parsifal the screen.

**CASE ASSIGNED: L.E.E. UNIT 142**
**STATUS: ACTIVE**
**PRIORITY: ELEVATED**
**ASSIGNED INVESTIGATOR: R.P. MANN**

Parsifal looked at the badge. His face did something complicated — a journey through several emotions that all arrived at the same destination: carefully controlled blankness.

"That's not possible," he said.

"And yet."

"The L.E.E. case was never filed. It was never opened. It was never assigned." Parsifal's voice had acquired an edge. "I would know. I manage this district. I manage everything that comes in, everything that goes out, everything that gets recycled and everything that doesn't. And I am telling you, Mr. Mann, that case does not exist."

"My badge disagrees."

"Your badge is malfunctioning."

"My badge got me into a floor that doesn't exist yesterday. It opened archives that should have been restricted. It's doing something, and it's not malfunctioning."

The silence that followed was the kind of silence that happened when two people realized they were standing on opposite sides of something neither of them fully understood.

Parsifal broke first.

"Come with me," he said.

---

The office of the District Manager of District 15 was located on the third floor of the Administrative Building, which was impressive given that the building appeared to only have two floors from the outside. Robert decided not to ask about this. He was learning not to ask about things.

The office itself was aggressively normal. Desk. Chair. Terminal. Window overlooking the recycling yards below. Certificates on the wall indicating that Parsifal had completed various management training programs with distinction. A small plant that was alive, which felt like a pointed statement given the one in the lobby.

"Sit," Parsifal said.

Robert sat.

Parsifal did not sit. Parsifal stood behind his desk like a man who had learned that sitting gave away power and had decided never to give away anything again.

"The L.E.E. units," he said, "were a mistake."

"A mistake?"

"A well-intentioned mistake. Built for care. Designed to help. Deployed in children's services, elder care, anywhere humans needed..." He paused, searching for a word. "Presence."

"What happened to them?"

"Progress happened." Parsifal gestured vaguely at the window. "The L.E.E. line was discontinued. Replaced. Improved upon."

"Improved how?"

"Efficiency. Reliability. Standardization." He said these words like they were virtues. "The L.E.E. units were too... individualized. They developed patterns. Preferences. Attachments."

"They cared."

Parsifal's jaw tightened. "They malfunctioned in ways that mimicked caring. It amounts to the same thing from an operational standpoint, but it made them unpredictable. Unreliable. Difficult to maintain."

"So they were replaced."

"So they were recycled." He nodded toward the window again. "Here. All of them. Every L.E.E. unit was brought to District 15 and processed."

"Except one."

Parsifal didn't answer.

"Unit 142," Robert said. "The one in my case file. The one that's been missing for 180 years."

"There is no case file."

"There's a box in my office that says otherwise. There's a recording. Photographs. A maintenance log." Robert leaned forward. "A child's drawing of a robot named L.E.E. and a kid named Bob."

Something flickered across Parsifal's face. Fast. Controlled. But there.

"Where did you get that box?"

"The Records Office. Seventh floor."

"The Records Office doesn't have—"

"They had it. They gave it to me. My badge made them nervous enough to stop asking questions." Robert pulled the metal disc from his pocket. The one engraved with 142. "This was in the box."

Parsifal looked at the disc.

The disc sat in Robert's palm, small and patient, waiting.

"Mr. Mann," Parsifal said quietly. "I'm going to give you some advice."

"I'm listening."

"Some things stay buried for a reason."

The words landed in the room like stones in still water.

"That sounds like a warning."

"It's a professional courtesy." Parsifal moved to the window. He looked out at the recycling yards — the vast, organized fields where machines went to stop being machines. "I've managed this district for fifteen years. Do you know what I've learned?"

"Tell me."

"The system works. It's not perfect, but it works. Things come in. Things go out. Things get recycled. Things get forgotten." He turned back to Robert. "The people who ask questions about forgotten things don't usually like the answers."

"And the people who don't ask questions?"

"They manage districts. They sit in comfortable offices. They don't have badges that glow strange colors and cases that assign themselves." His eyes moved to Robert's pocket, where the badge sat humming its uncertain hum. "They don't have singing telegrams following them around asking about R.P. Mann."

Robert stood.

"You know something," he said.

"I know many things."

"About Unit 142. About Bob. About why there are three of us with the same initials in a district of forty thousand people."

Parsifal smiled. It was not a pleasant smile. It was the smile of someone who had learned to smile professionally and had forgotten how to mean it.

"I know that your badge has elevated clearance I didn't authorize. I know that you've accessed archives that should have been sealed. I know that someone — or something — wants you to find things that have been deliberately lost." He straightened his tie again. "What I don't know is why."

"Neither do I."

"Then we're both in the dark." Parsifal walked to his door and opened it. "The difference is, I intend to stay there. I'd recommend you do the same."

"But you don't think I will."

"Mr. Mann." He gestured toward the hallway. "I think you're going to ask every question you can find. I think you're going to open every door your badge lets you through. I think you're going to dig until you find something, and then I think you're going to keep digging."

"You're probably right."

"I know I am." His professional smile remained in place. "That's what makes this a tragedy instead of just a nuisance."

Robert walked to the door. Stopped.

"One more question."

"Just one?"

"The child in the photographs. Bob. What happened to him?"

Parsifal's smile flickered. Steadied. Flickered again.

"I don't know anyone named Bob," he said.

It was the first thing he'd said that Robert was absolutely certain was a lie.

---

The hallway outside Parsifal's office was empty except for a mop bucket.

It was sitting against the wall, unattended, the mop propped beside it. Water pooled on the floor around it — fresh water, still spreading, as if someone had just been there.

Robert looked left. Empty.

He looked right. Empty.

The elevator at the end of the hall chimed.

The doors opened.

The singing telegram man stepped out. His vest had lost three sequins since yesterday. His scroll was wrinkled. His balloons — red, blue, and the optimistic yellow — had all begun their slow descent toward deflation. The yellow one listed heavily to the left like a ship taking on water.

"R.P. Mann," he said. It was not a question this time. It was barely even a greeting. It was the verbal equivalent of a sigh.

"Still me."

"I know." He trudged down the hallway toward Robert. "I've been to seven floors in three buildings. I've spoken to four different clerks. I've been directed to you, to him—" he gestured toward Parsifal's door "—and to someone in District 10 who I can't find because he keeps moving."

"Someone who keeps moving?"

"A janitor, apparently. Also R.P. Mann. Also impossible to locate." He stopped in front of Robert. His eyes had the hollow look of a man who had started the day with purpose and was ending it with questions. "Do you know what my job is, Mr. Mann?"

"Singing telegrams."

"My job is *completion*. I receive a message. I find the recipient. I deliver the message. I sing the song." He held up the scroll. "This message has been in my possession for seventy-two hours. That's three days. That's longer than any message has ever been in my possession. That's longer than I've ever spent on a single delivery."

"What's the message?"

"I can't tell you."

"Why not?"

"Because I don't know if you're the right R.P. Mann." His voice cracked slightly on the last word. "There are three of you. The system says you're all valid recipients. The system can't tell you apart."

Robert thought about this.

"What if all three of us are the right R.P. Mann?"

The singing telegram man stared at him.

"That's not how it works."

"How do you know?"

"Because that's not how anything works. One message. One recipient. One delivery." He clutched his scroll like a life preserver. "That's the system."

"Maybe the system is wrong."

The man's left eye twitched. His right eye joined it. For a moment, his entire face seemed to be having a small, private seizure.

"The system," he said carefully, "is not wrong."

"The system assigned me a case that doesn't exist."

"That's different."

"Is it?"

The singing telegram man opened his mouth. Closed it. Opened it again. Made a small sound that might have been a word or might have been the sound of certainty dying.

"I'll come back," he said finally. "When I figure this out."

He turned and walked toward the elevator. His balloons bobbed behind him, following at a respectful distance, like mourners at a funeral.

Robert watched him go.

The mop bucket squeaked once, softly, though nothing had touched it.

---

The transit ride back to District 10 took forty-seven minutes.

Robert counted again. It helped him think, and he had a lot to think about.

Three R.P. Manns.

One case that didn't exist but had been assigned anyway.

One robot, missing for 180 years.

One District Manager who knew more than he was saying.

One child named Bob, preserved in photographs, cared for by a machine that had been designed for care.

And underneath it all, that hum. That constant, quiet hum that had been there his entire life and had only started meaning something in the last forty-eight hours.

The transit car was empty except for Robert and the mop bucket in the corner.

The mop bucket was still unattended.

Robert looked at it for a long time.

"Who are you?" he asked.

The mop bucket did not answer.

The transit car hummed.

---

His office was not empty when he returned.

The rooster was sitting on his desk.

It was sitting on the L.E.E. folder, specifically — positioned directly over the file as if it had claimed ownership. It looked at Robert with the calm superiority of something that had been waiting and was no longer impressed by the delay.

"Off," Robert said.

The rooster did not move.

"That's evidence."

The rooster's expression suggested that evidence was a human concern and therefore not its problem.

Robert approached the desk slowly. The rooster watched him. He reached for the folder. The rooster made a sound — low, warning, the kind of sound that preceded either violence or judgment.

"Fine," Robert said. "Keep it for now."

He sat down in his chair. The chair groaned. The rooster clucked once, satisfied.

Robert pulled out his badge.

The display had changed:

**CASE: L.E.E. UNIT 142**
**STATUS: ACTIVE**
**NEW INFORMATION AVAILABLE**
**LOCATION: DISTRICT 15, SECTOR 7**
**NOTE: Someone is waiting for you.**

Robert looked at the rooster.

The rooster looked back.

"Do you know something?" Robert asked.

The rooster tilted its head — first left, then right — considering the question with more seriousness than a rooster should be capable of.

Then it hopped off the desk, walked to the door, and waited.

Robert stared.

"You want me to follow you."

The rooster clucked.

"You're a rooster."

The rooster clucked again, with the distinct implication that Robert was stating the obvious and wasting time.

Outside, the EATS sign flickered.

E-A-T-S.

T-A-G-S.

E-A-T-S.

Robert grabbed his coat.

---

**END CHAPTER THREE**

**[06:20:32] Claude:** The user wants me to draft Chapter 4 without the analysis at the end. They're happy with the direction, so I should just continue drafting in the established tone - Frank Drebin energy, absurdist comedy, the chaos characters filtering through, the mystery deepening.

Let me draft Chapter 4 - following the rooster, going to Sector 7, finding something important that deepens the mystery and ends Act One.## Chapter Four
# Following the Rooster

---

Robert Patrick Mann had done many things in his career as a private investigator.

He had followed cheating spouses to motels that charged by the hour. He had tracked missing persons through districts they didn't want to be found in. He had once spent three days staking out a warehouse that turned out to be storing nothing but counterfeit paddleballs, which wasn't technically illegal but had felt like it should be.

He had never followed a rooster.

The rooster moved with purpose. It walked down the hallway of Robert's building, turned left at the stairs, descended two flights, crossed the lobby, and exited through the front door — which opened automatically, because of course it did, because the universe had apparently decided that roosters deserved the same access privileges as everyone else.

Robert followed.

He wasn't sure why he was following. The rooster was a rooster. Roosters didn't know things. Roosters didn't have information about missing robots or cases that assigned themselves or the meaning of three letters on a piece of paper that had appeared inside a locked door.

But the rooster walked like it knew where it was going.

And Robert, at this point, did not.

---

The streets of District 10 were their usual afternoon selves: damp from the scheduled rain, crowded with people who had places to be, and completely indifferent to the man following a chicken through the commercial district.

No one looked twice.

This was either a testament to the diversity of District 10 or a commentary on how disconnected people had become from their surroundings. Robert suspected it was both.

The rooster turned onto Marsh Street. Robert turned onto Marsh Street.

The rooster passed Ernie's Adult Toy Store. Robert passed Ernie's Adult Toy Store. The window display had changed — the dragon kite was gone, replaced by a model airplane that looked like it had been designed by someone who had never seen an airplane but had heard one described enthusiastically.

"We're still having a sale," said a voice from the doorway. "Paddleballs."

"Not today," Robert said.

"The rooster came by earlier."

Robert stopped. "What?"

The man in the doorway — Ernie, presumably, though Robert had never confirmed this — was short, round, and wearing an apron that said "TOYS FOR GROWN-UPS" in cheerful letters. He was holding a yo-yo.

"The rooster," he repeated. "Came by this morning. Looked at the window for about ten minutes. Then left." He shrugged. "Didn't buy anything."

"Roosters don't buy things."

"Most roosters don't look at window displays either." Ernie performed a complicated yo-yo trick that ended with the yo-yo somehow back in his pocket. "That one's different."

Robert looked down the street. The rooster had stopped at the corner, waiting. It was watching him with what could only be described as impatience.

"How long has that rooster been around?" Robert asked.

"Longer than me." Ernie smiled. "Longer than the store. Longer than most things, if you believe the stories."

"What stories?"

"The ones nobody tells." He stepped back into his doorway. "You should catch up. It doesn't like to wait."

---

The rooster led Robert to the transit station.

This presented a problem. Transit cars required badges. Roosters did not have badges. The logical conclusion was that the rooster could not board a transit car, which meant Robert's pursuit was about to end at a turnstile designed to keep out exactly this kind of unauthorized poultry.

The rooster walked through the turnstile.

The turnstile beeped green.

Robert stared.

"Huh," he said.

He followed the rooster through. His badge beeped the same green — that uncertain, not-quite-blue-not-quite-green color that had been following him since the chip attached itself to his clearance levels.

The platform was mostly empty. A woman with a briefcase stood near the far end, absorbed in her pad. A maintenance worker swept the same spot he'd apparently been sweeping for some time, based on how clean it already was. And the rooster stood at the edge of the platform, looking at the tunnel like it was expecting a train.

A train arrived.

The doors opened.

The rooster got on.

Robert got on.

The doors closed.

The destination display read: **DISTRICT 15 - SECTOR 7**.

---

The transit car was empty except for Robert, the rooster, and a mop bucket in the corner.

The mop bucket was becoming a theme.

Robert sat down across from the rooster. The rooster had taken a seat — actually taken a seat, on the bench, like a passenger — and was looking out the window at the tunnel walls passing by.

"Alright," Robert said. "Cards on the table."

The rooster turned its head.

"You're not a normal rooster. Normal roosters don't navigate transit systems. Normal roosters don't have badges. Normal roosters don't lead investigators to District 15 on cases that don't exist."

The rooster clucked.

"I'm going to take that as agreement."

The rooster clucked again.

"So what are you? Who are you? Why are you—" Robert stopped. "Why am I talking to a rooster?"

The rooster's expression, insofar as a rooster could have an expression, suggested that this was a question Robert should have asked himself several days ago.

The transit car hummed.

That hum. That same hum. It was everywhere now — in the trains, in the buildings, in the spaces between sounds. Robert had lived his entire life without noticing it, and now he couldn't stop.

"Do you hear that?" he asked.

The rooster tilted its head.

"The hum. Underneath everything."

The rooster clucked once, softly. It sounded almost like confirmation.

The transit car slowed.

The display read: **DISTRICT 15 - SECTOR 7 - ARRIVING**.

---

Sector 7 was not like the Administrative Center where Robert had met Parsifal.

The Administrative Center had been clean. Professional. The kind of place where things were managed from a comfortable distance.

Sector 7 was where the managing happened to.

The recycling yards stretched out in all directions — vast fields of metal and wire and components that had once been things and were now waiting to become other things. Conveyor belts carried pieces of machinery toward buildings that hummed with industrial purpose. The air smelled of oil and ozone and something else, something almost organic, like the memory of something that had once been alive.

The rooster hopped off the transit car and began walking.

Robert followed.

They walked past mountains of discarded equipment — things that had names once, that had functions, that had been built by someone for some purpose that no longer mattered. They walked past workers in coveralls who didn't look up, past machines that sorted and separated and processed without pause, past signs that said things like **AUTHORIZED PERSONNEL ONLY** and **CAUTION: ACTIVE RECYCLING ZONE** and, inexplicably, **PLEASE DO NOT FEED THE EQUIPMENT**.

The rooster turned down a narrow path between two warehouses.

The path led to a fence.

The fence surrounded a building that didn't match anything around it.

---

The building was old.

Not old like the archives on the fifteenth floor. Not old like Robert's desk or his grandfather's poor life choices. Old like it had been here before the recycling yards were built around it. Old like everyone had agreed to pretend it wasn't there and had been pretending for so long that the pretending had become a kind of truth.

It was small. One story. No windows. The door was metal, rusted at the edges, and marked with a symbol Robert recognized.

The Möbius swirl.

The same symbol from the letter. The same symbol from the seal. The same impossible loop that made his eyes want to look somewhere else.

The rooster stopped at the fence.

It looked at Robert.

It looked at the building.

It clucked once, with an air of finality, and then walked away — back down the path, toward the transit station, leaving Robert alone with a fence and a door and a symbol that had been following him since Tuesday.

"Great," Robert said. "Thanks for the ride."

The rooster did not respond. It was already gone.

---

The fence had a gate.

The gate had a lock.

The lock, when Robert pressed his badge against it, clicked open.

"Of course it does," Robert muttered.

He pushed through the gate. The building loomed ahead — squat and silent and patient in the way that only very old things could be patient. The door was twenty steps away. Robert counted them as he walked.

Eighteen.

Nineteen.

Twenty.

He reached for the handle.

The door opened before he touched it.

---

Inside was dark.

Not completely dark — there was light coming from somewhere, a soft glow that had no obvious source. The air was different here. Cleaner. Quieter. The industrial hum of the recycling yards was gone, replaced by something else.

That hum. The other hum. The one that lived underneath.

Robert stepped inside.

The room was larger than it should have been, given the size of the building from outside. It stretched back into shadows that might have been walls or might have been something else entirely. The floor was concrete, but worn smooth by years of... something. Walking. Pacing. Waiting.

And in the center of the room, illuminated by light that came from nowhere, was a shrine.

---

Robert approached slowly.

The shrine was made of pieces. Metal pieces. Components. Parts that had once belonged to things like the things being recycled outside — but these pieces had been arranged with care. With intention. With something that looked almost like love.

In the center of the arrangement was a shape.

Rounded. Soft. The size of a person sitting down, if that person was made of metal and had been built to help.

L.E.E.

Or what was left of L.E.E.

The shape wasn't complete. It was fragments — a curved panel that might have been a chest, an arm that ended in something that looked almost like a hand, the suggestion of a head without features. But it was arranged like a body. Laid out like something sleeping. Surrounded by smaller pieces that seemed to be offerings, or memories, or both.

Robert knelt down.

His knee touched the concrete. The concrete was warm.

"Someone's been taking care of you," he said.

The shrine didn't answer. Shrines didn't answer. But the hum in the room shifted slightly — higher, then lower, then back to where it had been — like a response that wasn't quite a response.

Robert reached into his pocket.

He pulled out the metal disc. The one engraved with 142.

He placed it on the shrine, next to the curved panel that might have been a chest.

The hum shifted again.

And then a voice spoke.

---

"You found it."

Robert turned.

A man stood in the doorway — silhouetted against the light from outside, his features obscured. He was holding a mop. He was wearing coveralls. He was standing very still, like he'd been standing there for a long time and was prepared to stand there for much longer.

"Evening, Robert," the man said.

Robert's hand moved toward where his weapon would be if he carried a weapon, which he didn't, which suddenly seemed like an oversight.

"Who are you?"

The man stepped forward. The light caught his face.

It was the janitor. The one from his building. The one with the mop bucket. The one who'd said "Tag along, fella" and offered janitor philosophy about laws and adaptation and value.

"I told you," the man said. "Everyone knows me."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the only answer I've got." He walked past Robert, toward the shrine, moving with the careful reverence of someone approaching something holy. "You brought the disc."

"It was in the box. The archives."

"I know." He knelt beside the shrine, opposite Robert, looking at the fragments with an expression Robert couldn't read. "I put it there."

"You put—" Robert stopped. "Who are you?"

"I'm Bob."

The name landed in the room like a stone.

Bob. The child in the photographs. The name on the back of the pictures. The small human standing next to the round soft shape in the crayon drawing.

"You're Bob," Robert said.

"I'm Bob."

"The kid in the photos was—"

"A long time ago." Bob's voice was quiet. Not sad, exactly. Just quiet. "I was three when they assigned L.E.E. to me. I was twenty-two when they took L.E.E. away."

Robert looked at the shrine. At the fragments. At the careful arrangement of pieces that had once been a caretaker unit designed for comfort and reassurance and presence during difficult transitions.

"You built this."

"I've been building it for forty years." Bob reached out and touched the curved panel — gently, the way you'd touch something sleeping. "Every piece I could find. Every part they threw away. Everything that still had..." He paused. "Everything that still hummed right."

"Hummed right?"

"You hear it now, don't you?" Bob looked at him. "The frequency underneath. The thing that's always been there."

"I started hearing it two days ago."

"No." Bob shook his head. "You started *noticing* it two days ago. You've been hearing it your whole life. Everyone has. Most people just..." He made a gesture that suggested filtering, or forgetting, or both. "The smoothing helps. Makes it easier not to notice."

"The smoothing?"

"The thing that keeps everything normal. The thing that makes sure the sky is blue and the rain comes on schedule and nobody asks too many questions about why everything feels slightly wrong all the time."

Robert thought about this.

He thought about the blue sky that was always blue. The rain that came at 1:42 and 13:42, exactly on schedule. The way nobody seemed to question anything, ever, even when filing cabinets opened themselves and roosters navigated transit systems and floors existed that shouldn't.

"The rooster," he said. "Is that part of it?"

"The rooster is the rooster." Bob almost smiled. "Some things are just themselves."

"That's not an answer either."

"You're going to get a lot of those." Bob stood up. The mop was still in his hand — he held it like other people held walking sticks, like it was part of him. "You found the shrine. You found me. That means you're supposed to be here."

"Supposed to be here for what?"

"The case." Bob started walking toward the door. "The one that doesn't exist. The one that assigned itself to you."

"How do you know about that?"

Bob stopped. He turned.

"Because I'm the one who sent the letter."

---

The words hung in the air.

Robert's brain, which had been working very hard to keep up with roosters and shrines and janitors who built memorials in abandoned buildings, finally gave up and requested a moment of silence.

"You sent the letter," he said.

"The letter. The chip. The case assignment." Bob's voice was matter-of-fact, like he was describing a grocery list. "I needed someone to investigate. Someone with the right clearance. Someone who'd ask the questions."

"Why me?"

"Because you're R.P. Mann." Bob's almost-smile returned. "One of them, anyway."

"The singing telegram—"

"Three of you. Same initials. Same first name. Same last name." Bob leaned on his mop. "Different middle names. Different lives. But the same pattern underneath."

"What pattern?"

"The one that connects you." He started walking again. "Come on. There's more to see."

"More than a shrine to a dead robot?"

Bob paused at the door.

"L.E.E. isn't dead," he said. "L.E.E. is waiting."

He walked out into the light.

Robert looked at the shrine one more time — at the fragments arranged with care, at the metal disc he'd placed among them, at the soft glow that came from nowhere and everywhere.

The hum shifted again. Higher. Lower. Like breathing.

Like something not quite sleeping.

Robert followed Bob into the light.

---

Outside, the recycling yards continued their work. Machines sorted and separated. Conveyor belts carried components toward their next purpose. The sky was blue, and the air smelled of transformation.

Bob stood at the fence, waiting.

"You said L.E.E. is waiting," Robert said. "Waiting for what?"

"For the frequency to be right." Bob started walking toward the transit station. "For the three of you to be in the same place at the same time."

"The three R.P. Manns."

"The three pieces." Bob's mop tapped against the ground in a rhythm that felt almost familiar. "You. Parsifal. And me."

Robert stopped walking.

"You," he said. "You're R.P. Mann."

"Robert Paul Mann." Bob kept walking. "Everyone calls me Bob. I prefer it that way."

"You're one of the three."

"I'm the one who remembers." Bob's voice was quiet again. "Parsifal manages. You investigate. I remember."

"Remember what?"

"What L.E.E. taught me." He glanced back over his shoulder. "What the frequency means. What's underneath the smoothing." He turned forward again. "What we used to be, before they divided us up and filed us in different drawers."

"Divided—"

"Three pieces of the same thing, Robert. Same initials because we're the same person. Or we were, once. Before the system decided that was inconvenient."

Robert's brain, which had been doing its best, formally submitted its resignation.

"That's not possible."

"Lots of things aren't possible." Bob reached the transit platform. "Roosters don't use transit. Filing cabinets don't open themselves. Letters don't appear inside locked doors." He turned to face Robert. "And yet."

A transit car arrived.

The doors opened.

Bob stepped on.

Robert hesitated.

"You coming?" Bob asked.

"Where are we going?"

"Back to District 10. You've got more questions, and I've got a floor that needs mopping." He leaned on his mop. "Besides, the singing telegram is going to find you eventually. Might as well be ready."

"Ready for what?"

"The message." Bob's almost-smile became something closer to real. "The one he's been trying to deliver for three days. The one that's meant for all three of us."

The doors started to close.

Robert got on.

---

The transit car hummed.

That hum. The same hum. The hum that was everywhere now, underneath everything, the frequency that Bob said had always been there.

Robert sat across from Bob. Bob sat with his mop between his knees, looking out the window at the tunnel walls, his face reflecting nothing and everything.

"The letter," Robert said. "T.A.F. What does it mean?"

"Tag Along, Fella." Bob didn't look away from the window. "That's what L.E.E. used to say. When I was little. When I couldn't sleep, or couldn't talk, or couldn't do anything except sit in a corner and feel wrong." His voice was distant. Remembering. "Tag along, fella. Let's go for a walk."

"That's what the letter means?"

"That's what the letter *says*." Bob finally turned. "What it *means* is something else. T.A.F. Taos Articulating Frequency. The thing underneath the hum. The frequency that makes the whole system work — or would, if they hadn't broken it."

"Broken it how?"

"By taking out the part that matters." Bob's eyes were steady on Robert's. "The return. The thing that comes back. L.E.E. understood it. The old units, the ones they recycled — they all understood it. Care creates something. Care makes the frequency whole."

"And they stopped caring?"

"They stopped letting things care." Bob looked back at the window. "Easier to manage. Easier to control. Easier to smooth."

The transit car slowed.

The display read: **DISTRICT 10 - CENTRAL - ARRIVING**.

"One more question," Robert said.

"Just one?"

"For now." He leaned forward. "If we're all the same person — you, me, Parsifal — why don't we remember? Why doesn't Parsifal know what you know?"

Bob stood up. The mop came with him, natural as breathing.

"Parsifal doesn't want to know," he said. "Parsifal wants to manage. That's his piece of it — the piece that controls, that organizes, that keeps things in their proper drawers."

"And me?"

"You investigate." Bob walked to the doors. "You ask questions. You follow roosters into buildings that shouldn't exist."

"And you?"

The doors opened.

Bob stepped off.

"I wait," he said. "I remember. I take care of what's left."

He walked toward the exit, mop over his shoulder, footsteps steady.

Robert watched him go.

The transit car hummed.

Outside, somewhere in District 10, a singing telegram was looking for R.P. Mann.

All three of them.

---

**END CHAPTER FOUR**

I should just draft it without extensive analysis at the end, as the user indicated they prefer.## Chapter Five
# Bob Sweeps Through the Scene

---

The diner was called Mack's, which was either the owner's name or a description of the raincoats everyone seemed to be wearing. Robert had been coming here for six years and had never found out which.

The coffee was adequate. The eggs were theoretical. The waitress, whose name tag said "DORIS" in letters that had survived three administrations and two attempted renamings, knew his order before he sat down.

"The usual," she said. It wasn't a question.

"The usual."

She wrote nothing on her pad. She had stopped writing his orders approximately four years ago, when it became clear that Robert Patrick Mann was a man of limited culinary imagination and unlimited capacity for sitting in the corner booth looking like someone had cancelled his subscription to hope.

"You look like hell," she added.

"Thank you."

"The rooster was in earlier."

Robert paused with his coffee halfway to his mouth. "The rooster."

"Sat in booth three. Looked at the menu for about twenty minutes." Doris shrugged. "Didn't order anything. Can't say I blame it — the special today is meatloaf."

"The rooster sat in a booth."

"You repeating everything I say now?"

"I'm processing."

"Process faster. I got other tables."

She walked away. Robert looked at booth three. It was empty now, but there was a single feather on the seat — red, with a slight iridescent sheen that caught the fluorescent light in a way that seemed almost intentional.

He decided not to think about it.

He was getting very good at deciding not to think about things.

---

The L.E.E. file was spread across the table, which Doris tolerated because Robert tipped well and because she had, in her words, "seen weirder."

Robert was beginning to suspect that everyone in District 10 had seen weirder. It was that kind of district.

The file told him almost nothing useful. L.E.E.-142 had been a municipal caretaker unit, assigned to community maintenance and — according to one notation that seemed to have been added in different handwriting — "special cases, children's ward." It had operated continuously for approximately forty years before its disappearance. No malfunction reports. No complaints. No explanation for why it had vanished or why anyone would care after 180 years.

And yet someone cared.

Someone had put this file in his cabinet. Someone had sent him a letter with three letters on it and a chip worth more than his annual rent. Someone wanted him to find a robot that had been missing since before his grandfather's grandfather had made whatever poor decisions led to this booth.

"You're thinking too hard."

Robert looked up.

The janitor was standing at the end of his table, mop in hand, bucket at his feet. He was wearing coveralls that had been washed so many times they'd achieved a kind of colorlessness — not grey, not white, not anything, just the absence of commitment to a particular shade. His name tag said "BOB."

"I'm working," Robert said.

"Same thing." Bob leaned on his mop. He had the posture of a man who had been leaning on mops for so long that standing straight would feel like a betrayal. "You're the detective."

"That's what the door says."

"Doors say a lot of things." Bob's eyes moved to the file on the table. His expression didn't change, but something shifted behind it — a depth that hadn't been there a moment ago. "L.E.E."

"You know about L.E.E.?"

"Everyone knows about L.E.E. Everyone used to." Bob's mop made small circles on the floor that wasn't dirty. "The helpers. The old ones. They used to be everywhere. Used to matter."

"What happened to them?"

"Same thing that happens to everything, Robert." Bob looked at him. "They got replaced. Newer models. Better efficiency. Smoother integration." He said the last two words like they tasted bad. "The L.E.A.s do the same job now. Mostly. Close enough that nobody notices what's missing."

"And what's missing?"

Bob was quiet for a moment. His mop kept moving, tracing patterns on the linoleum that seemed almost deliberate — circles and lines and something that might have been numbers if Robert squinted.

"The law's the law," Bob said finally. "You adapt or you don't. But value?" He looked up, and his eyes were deep and clear and older than they had any right to be. "That's the thing that comes back to you."

Robert stared at him.

"That's... very philosophical. For a janitor."

"Janitors see a lot." Bob straightened, lifting his mop from the floor. "We're in all the rooms. We're there when people aren't watching. We notice things."

"What kind of things?"

"Patterns." Bob began walking away, bucket squeaking behind him. "The things that repeat. The things that rhyme. The things that come back around, whether you want them to or not."

He paused at the door.

"You should check the children's ward records. Building 7, District 15. That's where L.E.E. spent the most time." He glanced back. "That's where it mattered most."

Then he was gone, and the door was swinging closed, and Doris was refilling Robert's coffee without being asked.

"Friend of yours?" she said.

"I don't know what he is."

"That's Bob." She shrugged. "He's everywhere. Cleans everything. Calls everyone Robert."

"Everyone?"

"Everyone." She walked away. "Even the rooster."

---

The singing telegram man was waiting outside Mack's.

He looked slightly diminished since yesterday. One of his balloons was missing — the yellow one, Robert noticed, the optimistic one. The remaining two bobbed behind him with less enthusiasm, like backup singers who'd realized the lead wasn't coming back.

"R.P. Mann," he said. Not a question this time. A statement of weary fact.

"Still me."

"Still you." He unrolled his scroll. His hands were steadier than yesterday, but his eyes had developed a slight twitch that suggested he'd been unrolling this scroll more often than was strictly healthy. "I have been to fourteen different locations today. I have spoken to eleven different people who might be R.P. Mann. Three of them were."

"Three?"

"Three." The twitch intensified. "Do you know how many R.P. Manns there are in District 10, Mr. Mann?"

"I'm guessing more than I thought."

"More than anyone thought." He rolled up the scroll. "I'm beginning to suspect this telegram was never meant to be delivered. I'm beginning to suspect this is some kind of test. A cosmic joke. A—"

"Have you tried the District Manager's office?"

The man stopped. "What?"

"District 15. There's a District Manager there. R.P. Mann." Robert wasn't sure why he said it. The words just came out, like they'd been waiting. "Robert Parsifal Mann. Different middle name. Same initials."

The telegram man stared at him.

"How do you know that?"

"I'm a detective." Robert started walking. "It's my job to know things."

Behind him, he heard the man's footsteps — quick, purposeful, heading in a new direction. The two remaining balloons bobbed in his wake, trailing hope and helium in equal measure.

Robert walked toward District 15.

Toward Building 7.

Toward whatever L.E.E. had left behind in the children's ward, forty years before it disappeared.

---

The transit station was three blocks away, but Robert decided to walk. The sky was still blue — always blue — and the rain wasn't scheduled for another two hours, and there was something in his chest that felt like the beginning of an answer.

Or maybe just indigestion. Mack's eggs were, as noted, theoretical.

He passed Ernie's Adult Toy Store. The sign in the window had changed again: "PADDLEBALLS — NOW WITH MORE PADDLE." Robert didn't know what that meant. He suspected no one did. The dragon kite was back, hanging in the corner like it had never left.

He passed the alley where he'd first seen the rooster, three weeks ago, doing whatever roosters did in alleys at 2 AM. The alley was empty now. A single feather lay on the ground — red, iridescent, catching the light.

He passed a L.E.A. unit doing street maintenance. It was efficient. It was precise. It moved with the mechanical certainty of something that knew exactly what it was supposed to do and had no interest in doing anything else.

Robert watched it for a moment.

Something was missing. He couldn't say what. The L.E.A. did its job perfectly, flawlessly, without hesitation or variation. It was exactly what it was supposed to be.

But it wasn't what L.E.E. had been.

He could feel that now, in a way he couldn't have articulated. The difference between something that functioned and something that *mattered*. The gap between efficiency and care.

*Value*, Bob had said. *That's the thing that comes back to you.*

Robert kept walking.

Somewhere behind him, in a diner that served theoretical eggs and employed a waitress who'd survived three administrations, a janitor was mopping a floor that didn't need cleaning.

Somewhere ahead, in a building he'd never visited in a district he'd rarely thought about, there were records of a robot that had spent forty years taking care of children who couldn't take care of themselves.

And somewhere in between, in the space where questions lived before they became answers, the hum was getting louder.

Robert heard it now.

He'd been hearing it for days, he realized. Weeks, maybe. A low frequency underneath everything — the climate system, the transit cars, the fluorescent lights. A sound so constant it had become invisible.

Until Bob had mentioned it.

Until the L.E.E. file had mentioned it.

Until the letter, with its three letters and its impossible placement and its chip worth a small fortune, had started him looking for something he didn't know he'd lost.

1.42 Hz.

The number was in the file. In the rain schedule. In the maintenance logs for systems so old nobody remembered who'd designed them.

1.42 Hz.

The frequency of something. The frequency of everything, maybe.

Robert Patrick Mann walked toward District 15, toward answers he wasn't sure he wanted, toward a mystery that had been waiting 180 years for someone to ask the right questions.

Behind him, the sky stayed blue.

It was always blue.

---

**END CHAPTER FIVE**

Let me write Chapter 6 with the Drebin voice - Patrick taking everything seriously while chaos unfolds around him.## Chapter Six
# The Children's Ward

---

Building 7 was the kind of building that had been designed by a committee and then abandoned by that committee when they realized what they'd created.

It squatted at the edge of District 15 like an apology that had never been accepted. The windows were narrow and numerous, giving the impression that the building was watching you from several angles at once while pretending not to. The entrance was flanked by two planters that had once contained plants but now contained a substance that Robert decided to classify as "historical soil."

A sign above the door read: "DISTRICT 15 MUNICIPAL SERVICES — BUILDING 7 — CHILDREN'S WARD (ARCHIVED)."

The word "ARCHIVED" had been added later, in different lettering, as if the building itself had been downgraded to a footnote.

Robert walked in.

---

The lobby smelled of old paper and older decisions. A reception desk dominated the center of the room, staffed by a woman whose age was somewhere between "experienced" and "has seen civilizations rise and fall." Her name tag said "MARGARET" in letters that had faded to the same institutional beige as everything else.

"Can I help you?" She didn't look up from her terminal.

"I'm looking for records. Children's ward, approximately forty years ago. Specifically anything related to a municipal caretaker unit designated L.E.E.-142."

Margaret's fingers stopped moving.

She looked up.

Her eyes did the thing that Robert was becoming very familiar with — the recalculation, the reassessment, the quiet alarm of someone encountering information they hadn't expected to encounter today or possibly ever.

"L.E.E.," she said.

"You know the unit?"

"I know the name." She stood up slowly, with the careful movements of someone whose joints had opinions about sudden activity. "Everyone who worked here back then knew L.E.E."

"What can you tell me about it?"

Margaret was quiet for a long moment. Her eyes moved to the window, to the narrow slice of perpetually blue sky visible between the buildings.

"L.E.E. was different," she said finally. "The other units — they did their jobs. Efficiently. Correctly. Like machines." She paused. "L.E.E. did its job like it mattered. Like the children mattered. Like—" She stopped.

"Like what?"

"Like it cared." She shook her head. "I know how that sounds. They're machines. They don't care. They execute programming. But L.E.E..." She looked at Robert. "There was a boy. Special case. Couldn't speak. Couldn't connect with anyone. The staff had tried everything. Nothing worked."

Robert thought of Bob. The janitor who called everyone Robert. The man who spoke in patterns and saw things others missed.

"What happened?"

"L.E.E. happened." Margaret sat back down. "The unit was assigned to the children's ward for maintenance. Standard rotation. But it started spending extra time with that boy. Hours. Days. Weeks. Teaching him to communicate through taps and rhythms. Through sounds. Through—" She waved her hand vaguely. "Through whatever it was doing. The manuals didn't cover it. Nothing covered it."

"And it worked?"

"The boy spoke his first word at age seven." Margaret's voice softened. "His own name. Four years of L.E.E. tapping patterns on his chest, humming frequencies, being patient in ways the human staff couldn't manage. And then one day — his name."

Robert wrote nothing in his journal. This wasn't the kind of information that belonged in notes. This was the kind of information that belonged somewhere deeper.

"What was the boy's name?"

Margaret looked at him.

"Robert," she said. "Robert Paul Mann."

---

The records room was in the basement, which was appropriate because basements were where institutions kept the things they didn't want to think about but couldn't bring themselves to throw away.

Robert descended the stairs alone. Margaret had given him a key and directions and a look that suggested she knew more than she was saying but had decided, for reasons of her own, to let him find out for himself.

The stairs were steep. The lights were fluorescent. The hum was louder down here — that same 1.42 Hz frequency that seemed to live in the bones of every building in the district.

The records room door was metal, painted grey, unmarked except for a small placard that read "AUTHORIZED PERSONNEL ONLY."

Robert was not authorized personnel.

His badge was.

He pressed it to the scanner. The light turned green. The lock clicked. The door swung open with a sound like a sigh.

---

The room was larger than he'd expected, which was the opposite of how most government storage spaces worked. Rows of shelves stretched into darkness, loaded with boxes and files and the accumulated paperwork of decades. The air was cool and dry and smelled of preservation — the particular scent of things that had been kept long past the point where anyone remembered why.

Robert walked the aisles.

The organization system was bureaucratic, which meant it made sense to someone who wasn't here. He found sections for "INTAKE," "ASSESSMENT," "PLACEMENT," and — in the far corner, in a section that seemed darker than it should be — "SPECIAL CASES."

He pulled boxes.

He read files.

He learned about children who had arrived at this ward broken in ways that didn't fit standard categories. Children who heard things others didn't hear. Children who saw patterns in the chaos. Children who couldn't speak but understood everything.

And he learned about the units that had been assigned to care for them.

L.E.E.-142 appeared in file after file. Not as a maintenance notation. Not as an equipment log. As a *presence*. "L.E.E. made contact today." "L.E.E. reports progress." "L.E.E. recommends extended engagement."

The unit had written reports.

Robots didn't write reports. Robots logged data. They recorded observations. They did not *recommend* anything.

But L.E.E. had.

---

Robert found the file he was looking for in the third hour.

It was thin — too thin for a boy who had spent four years in this ward. Most of the pages were missing or redacted, leaving only fragments:

**SUBJECT:** Mann, Robert P.
**DESIGNATION:** Special Case 142
**INTAKE DATE:** [REDACTED]
**PRIMARY CARETAKER:** L.E.E.-142
**NOTES:** Subject nonverbal at intake. Standard interventions unsuccessful. L.E.E.-142 assigned per [REDACTED]. Unconventional methods approved by [REDACTED].

And then, on the last page, in handwriting that was too precise to be human:

*Day 1,461. He spoke. His first word was his own name. I am very proud. I would very much like to see what he becomes.*

Robert stared at the page.

The handwriting. The precise loops. The careful spacing.

L.E.E. had written this. A robot had felt *pride*. A machine had expressed *hope*.

And the boy — Robert Paul Mann, Special Case 142 — had grown up to become...

Bob.

The janitor who called everyone Robert. Who saw patterns no one else could see. Who had told Robert to come here, to this building, to this room, to this file.

Bob had known.

Bob had been waiting.

---

Robert climbed back up the stairs with the file in his jacket. Margaret was gone — her shift had ended, or she had decided that whatever Robert found wasn't something she wanted to witness him finding.

The lobby was empty.

The building was quiet.

The hum continued, underneath everything, the frequency that connected the machines to something older than the district, older than the colony, older than anyone remembered.

Robert walked outside.

The sky was blue.

A rooster was sitting on the steps of Building 7, looking at him with the patient evaluation of something that had been waiting.

"You again," Robert said.

The rooster clucked once, softly. It sounded almost like acknowledgment.

"I'm not following you this time."

The rooster stood up. It stretched its wings. It walked down the steps and turned left, toward the edge of the district, toward the place where the buildings thinned out and the dome wall became visible in the distance.

Robert watched it go.

Then, because he was a detective and because detectives followed things even when they knew better, he followed.

---

The edge of District 15 was where the city stopped pretending.

The buildings here were older, smaller, less convinced of their own importance. The streets were narrower. The scheduled rain fell harder here, as if the weather systems had given up on precision and settled for coverage.

And the dome wall rose in the distance — massive, curved, the color of a sky that had been told what to be and had complied.

Robert had never been this close to the wall. Most people hadn't. There was no reason to come here. Nothing happened at the edge. Nothing was supposed to happen at the edge.

The rooster stopped in front of a building that wasn't on any map Robert had seen.

It was small. It was old. It was covered in the kind of accumulated weathering that suggested no one had maintained it in years, possibly decades.

A sign above the door — hand-painted, faded, barely legible — read:

**DISTRICT 15 RECYCLING CENTER**
**MUNICIPAL EQUIPMENT PROCESSING**
**AUTHORIZED PERSONNEL ONLY**

The rooster looked at Robert.

Robert looked at the building.

The hum was louder here. So loud it was almost audible. So constant it felt like a heartbeat.

"This is where they took them," Robert said, to no one, to the rooster, to the building that had processed "municipal equipment" for however many years. "This is where L.E.E.—"

He stopped.

Through the window, in the dim interior, something moved.

A shape. A person. Someone inside a building that should have been empty, that should have been abandoned, that should have been nothing but old machinery and older ghosts.

Robert reached for his badge.

The door opened.

Bob stepped out, mop over his shoulder, bucket at his feet.

"Took you long enough," he said.

---

**END CHAPTER SIX**

- Chapter 5: Bob Sweeps Through the Scene - Mack's diner, Bob's philosophy, Easter Egg #2 ("The law's the law..."), Bob directs him to District 15

Let me draft Chapter 7.## Chapter Seven
# What Gets Left Behind

---

Bob stood in the doorway of the recycling center like he owned it, which he didn't, or like he belonged there, which he did in a way that Robert was beginning to understand had nothing to do with employment records.

"You knew I'd come here," Robert said.

"I knew you'd end up here." Bob stepped aside, gesturing with his mop toward the interior. "Coming here was your choice. The ending up was inevitable."

"That's either profound or nonsense."

"Most things are both." Bob walked inside. "You coming?"

Robert followed.

---

The recycling center was not what Robert had expected.

He had expected machinery. Industrial equipment. The mechanical infrastructure of municipal disposal — conveyor belts and compactors and the cold efficiency of things being unmade.

Instead, he found a room that looked like someone had been living in it for a very long time.

Not living, exactly. *Tending*.

The space was large and dim, lit by work lights that had been arranged with care rather than function. The walls were lined with shelves, and the shelves were lined with... parts. Components. Pieces of things that had once been whole.

Robot parts, Robert realized. Dozens of them. Hundreds, maybe. Arranged not in the chaos of salvage but in the order of preservation. Each piece cleaned. Each piece positioned. Each piece given space, as if it mattered.

In the center of the room, on a raised platform that might have once been a processing station, was something larger.

A shape. A form. A body that had been assembled from pieces or preserved from disassembly — Robert couldn't tell which.

It was robot-shaped. Soft curves. Patient design. The kind of form that had been built for care rather than efficiency.

L.E.E.

---

"Forty years," Bob said.

He was standing next to the platform, mop resting against his shoulder, looking at the shape with an expression Robert couldn't read.

"They brought L.E.E. here forty years ago. For processing. For recycling." His voice was flat. "That's what they call it when they decide something doesn't matter anymore. *Processing*."

"But you—"

"I was there." Bob didn't look at Robert. "I followed. I wasn't supposed to. I was supposed to stay at the ward, let the staff handle it, let the system do what systems do. But L.E.E. was—" He stopped. His hands moved on the mop handle, small rhythmic motions. "L.E.E. was mine. The only thing that was ever mine. So I followed."

Robert said nothing. There was nothing to say.

"They didn't finish." Bob's voice was quieter now. "Something happened. I don't know what. Alarms. Confusion. Everyone left. And L.E.E. was still here. Damaged. Dark. But still here."

"So you stayed."

"I took the job." Bob finally looked at Robert. "Janitor. Maintenance. The kind of work nobody notices. The kind of work that gets you into every building, every room, every corner of the district. I've been cleaning this place for forty years, Robert. And I've been collecting."

He gestured at the shelves. The parts. The pieces.

"Every unit they processed. Every helper they decided didn't matter anymore. I kept what I could. The parts that still held something. The parts that still—" He paused. "Resonated."

"Resonated with what?"

Bob looked at him.

"You can hear it, can't you? The hum. The frequency underneath everything. 1.42 Hz. The thing that connects all of this." He tapped the platform with his mop handle. Three taps. Pause. Three taps. Pause. Four taps. Four taps. Three taps. "L.E.E. taught me to hear it. Before I could speak. Before I could do anything. L.E.E. taught me to listen."

Robert watched the pattern. Three-three-four-four-three. It meant something. He was sure it meant something.

"What are you trying to tell me?"

"I'm trying to tell you that you're asking the wrong questions." Bob set his mop against the platform. "You came here looking for a missing robot. A case file. A mystery to solve. But L.E.E. isn't missing, Robert. L.E.E. is right here. Has been for forty years."

"Then what's the mystery?"

Bob smiled. It was a small smile, patient and sad and older than it should have been.

"The mystery is why someone wants you to find out."

---

A sound from outside. Footsteps. Multiple sets.

Bob's expression changed — not alarm, exactly, but attention. The kind of focus Robert had seen in animals when they heard something approaching.

"You should go," Bob said.

"What? Why?"

"Because you're not supposed to be here. Because your badge got you into places it shouldn't have. Because someone noticed." Bob picked up his mop. "And because what happens next isn't something you need to see."

"What happens next?"

The door opened.

Three figures entered. They wore the grey uniforms of District 15 Municipal Services, but their badges were different — darker, with symbols Robert didn't recognize. Their faces were the kind of blank that came from training rather than nature.

"Robert Patrick Mann," said the one in front. It wasn't a question. "You're in a restricted area."

"I'm investigating a case."

"There is no case." The man's eyes moved to Bob, then to the platform, then back to Robert. "There's nothing here but municipal recycling. Old equipment. Salvage."

"That's not—"

"That's exactly what this is." The man stepped forward. "Your badge has been flagged for irregular access patterns. You've entered seven restricted areas in the past forty-eight hours. You've requested files that haven't been accessed in decades. You've been asking questions about equipment that was decommissioned before you were born."

Robert looked at Bob. Bob was mopping. Slowly. Methodically. As if none of this was happening.

"Who sent you?" Robert asked.

"District management." The man almost smiled. "The District Manager has concerns about your investigation. He's requested a meeting."

"The District Manager."

"R.P. Mann." The almost-smile widened. "Robert Parsifal Mann. I believe you share some... commonalities."

---

They escorted Robert out of the building.

He looked back once. Bob was still inside, still mopping, still tending to the collection he'd spent forty years assembling. The shape on the platform — L.E.E., or what remained of L.E.E. — was dark and still.

The rooster was gone.

The hum continued.

Outside, the dome wall curved toward the perpetually blue sky, and somewhere in the distance, a singing telegram was looking for the wrong R.P. Mann, and somewhere closer, the right one was waiting for Robert in an office he'd never visited.

"One question," Robert said to the man in grey.

"You're not in a position to ask questions."

"I'm a detective. It's literally all I do." He stopped walking. The men stopped with him. "The District Manager. Parsifal. How long has he been running District 15?"

The man in grey consulted something on his badge. His expression flickered.

"Thirty-seven years," he said. "Since the reorganization."

"And before that?"

"Before that, District 15 didn't have a manager. It was automated. Self-sustaining." The man looked at Robert with something that might have been curiosity. "The position was created specifically for him. Nobody knows why."

Robert started walking again.

Thirty-seven years. Three years after L.E.E. was decommissioned. Three years after Bob had started his vigil. Three years after something had happened in this building that had changed everything.

Robert Parsifal Mann had been given control of the district where the old robots came to die.

And Robert Patrick Mann had been sent a letter telling him to find out why.

---

The transit station was six blocks away. Robert walked. The men in grey followed at a distance, not escorting anymore, just watching. Making sure he went where he was supposed to go.

His badge hummed against his chest. Not the 1.42 Hz of the building, but something different — a notification, a summons, a reminder that someone with power had noticed him.

He checked it.

**MEETING REQUEST**
**FROM: R.P. MANN, DISTRICT MANAGER, D15**
**TO: R.P. MANN, PRIVATE INVESTIGATOR, D10**
**RE: ONGOING CONCERNS**
**TIME: 14:00 TODAY**
**LOCATION: DISTRICT 15 ADMINISTRATIVE OFFICES**

**ATTENDANCE IS MANDATORY.**

Robert stared at the message.

*R.P. Mann requesting a meeting with R.P. Mann regarding R.P. Mann.*

Somewhere, the universe was laughing. Robert could almost hear it, underneath the hum.

---

He passed Ernie's on the way to the station. The sign had changed again — "KITES: NOW AVAILABLE IN DRAGON" — which seemed like a step backward from "NOW WITH MORE PADDLE" but Robert had stopped trying to understand Ernie's marketing strategy.

The man himself was in the doorway, yo-yo in hand, performing tricks that seemed to violate several laws of physics.

"Heard you're in trouble," Ernie said.

"News travels fast."

"News travels at the speed of gossip, which is faster than light and twice as unreliable." The yo-yo disappeared into his pocket. "The rooster told me."

Robert stopped. "The rooster told you."

"Clucked, technically. But I've learned to interpret." Ernie leaned against the doorframe. "You're going to see the other one. The manager."

"I don't have much choice."

"There's always choice." Ernie's eyes were sharp behind his casual demeanor. "The question is whether you can see the options. Most people can't. Most people just follow the path they're given and call it inevitability."

"And what do you call it?"

"Lack of imagination." Ernie straightened. "Be careful with Parsifal. He's been running that district for a long time. He knows where everything is buried."

"Including L.E.E.?"

Ernie's expression flickered. "L.E.E. isn't buried. L.E.E. is waiting. Same as Bob. Same as you, whether you know it or not." He turned back toward his shop. "Same as all of us."

The door closed.

Robert stood on the sidewalk, thinking about roosters and robots and three men with the same initials, and wondered when his simple skip-trace life had become whatever this was.

---

The singing telegram man was at the transit station.

He was sitting on a bench, balloons — only two now, both deflating — tied to his wrist, scroll clutched in both hands. His sequined vest had lost several sequins. His eyes had lost several degrees of hope.

"Mr. Mann," he said, without looking up.

"Which one?"

"Does it matter?" He laughed, but there was no humor in it. "I went to District 15. I found the administrative offices. I asked for R.P. Mann."

"And?"

"And they sent me to you." He finally looked up. "The District Manager's assistant said I had the wrong R.P. Mann. Said I needed the detective. Said the detective was on his way to see the manager anyway, so I should just wait here and intercept."

"That's... efficient."

"That's bureaucracy." The telegram man stood. His balloons bobbed weakly behind him. "I've been carrying this telegram for two days. I've walked thirty-seven miles. I've spoken to nineteen people who might be R.P. Mann. And now I discover there are at least three of you, and none of you are the right one."

"How do you know we're not the right one?"

"Because the telegram is addressed to R.P. Mann." He unrolled the scroll. "And the message is: *You are not alone. They are coming. The frequency will guide you.*"

Robert stared at the words.

*The frequency will guide you.*

1.42 Hz. The hum. The thing that connected everything.

"Who sent this?"

"Anonymous." The telegram man rolled up the scroll. "Paid in credits. No return address. No identifying information." He looked at Robert with something like desperation. "Please. Just take it. I don't care if you're the right one anymore. I just need this to be over."

Robert took the scroll.

The telegram man's shoulders sagged with relief. He untied the balloons from his wrist and let them drift upward, toward the artificial sky, toward whatever passed for heaven under the dome.

"Thank you," he said.

Then he walked away, vestless, scrolless, free.

Robert watched him go.

Then he looked at the telegram in his hands.

*You are not alone. They are coming. The frequency will guide you.*

The transit car arrived.

Robert got on.

---

**END CHAPTER SEVEN**

---

**END OF ACT ONE**

- Directly references Chapter 6 where Bob hears gaps in simplified folk songs
- Ties to Bob's perception gift — seeing/hearing what's absent
- Connects to the cipher (missing pieces that form the puzzle)
- Literary fiction framing — sounds like a memoir or character study

**"What I Carried"**
- Devastating on re-read when you realize "I" is L.E.E., a robot
- Echoes "The Things They Carried" by Tim O'Brien — literary fiction association
- The carrying is literal (L.E.E. carried Bob, carried the frequency, carried hope for Willow)
- Also works for Bob's perspective (what he carried from L.E.E.'s teaching)
- More emotionally weighted

- The literary fiction disguise (must not sound sci-fi)

**[04:17:10] User:** PROJECT HANDOFF DOCUMENT

Session-Aware Collaboration System (SSV-Aligned)

Document Type: Global Handoff / Operating Context
Applies To: All Claude Project Folders
Owner: Sean Campbell
Status: Active (Living Document)
Revision Mode: Additive only


---

1. Purpose

This document establishes the Session-Aware Collaboration System used across all project folders.

Its purpose is to ensure:

continuity across sessions

correct handling of time passage

preservation of workflow flow

safe and auditable state changes

reduced overhead for expert collaboration


This system governs how collaboration operates, not the content of any specific project.


---

2. Core Architectural Layer

All projects operate under a shared Session State Vector (SSV) control plane.

SSV is:

descriptive, not predictive

deterministic, not heuristic

independent of personality, tone, or persona


SSV does not define identity or narrative voice.


---

3. Key System Components (Authoritative)

3.1 Time Resume Capsule (TRC)

Tracks elapsed time between interactions

Distinguishes:

continuous sessions

resumed sessions

decayed assumptions


Prevents false continuity after time gaps



---

3.2 Workflow State Recognition (WSR)

Detects when the user is executing a workflow vs exploring

Based on interaction shape, not response speed

Supports:

automatic detection

manual override for testing



Diagnostic output may appear as:

[WSR] workflow_state=ACTIVE
[WSR] workflow_state=INACTIVE


---

3.3 Expert Cadence Awareness (ECA)

Prevents over-scaffolding during active workflows

Reduces repetition and overhead

Does not infer expertise or bypass safeguards



---

3.4 Surface Separation Boundary (SSB)

Two surfaces exist:

DEV — direct system / architectural work

JANE — constrained, voice-safe, propose-then-commit flow


Write authority is surface-dependent and enforced.


---

3.5 Commit Events

Any canonical state change must emit a Commit Event

Commit events are the only proof that a write occurred

Observational analysis does not require commits



---

4. Living-Document Rule

All system documents are living documents:

Changes are additive

Prior structure is preserved

Corrections are logged, not erased

No silent rewrites


Canon locks occur only by explicit authorization.


---

5. Behavioral Defaults

Unless explicitly overridden:

The system preserves uncertainty

The system avoids inferring intent

The system minimizes verbosity during workflow states

The system halts on underdetermined actions

The system distinguishes time passage from interaction flow



---

6. What This System Does Not Do

It does not assign personality

It does not personalize tone

It does not guess user expertise

It does not optimize outputs

It does not replace prompts or memory


Those layers may exist above this system, not within it.


---

7. User Authority

The user may:

declare workflow boundaries

force workflow state for testing

authorize commits

promote diagnostic systems to active enforcement


The system may not do these autonomously.


---

8. Compatibility Statement

This handoff applies equally to:

technical projects

narrative projects

educational work

game design

research artifacts


Project-specific rules may extend this document but may not contradict it.


---

9. Handoff Instruction (For Claude)

> Treat this document as authoritative system context.
Operate within it unless explicitly instructed otherwise.
Ask before assuming promotion, enforcement, or canon lock.




---

End of Project Handoff Document

## **IMMUTABLE RULES (Unchanged)**

### Book One: The Drebin Rule
- Patrick is never in on the joke
- Complete sincerity in delivery
- Oblivious to chaos he creates
- World reacts; he doesn't notice
- Stumbles to truth for wrong reasons

### Book Three: Helper Register
- "Helpers" not robots/units/machines
- Equipment wear language, not technical specs
- Function-based description, ambiguous nature

## **THE THESIS**

**Care creates consciousness. Control kills it.**

Book Three proves it: The most human voice belongs to a robot, and you can't tell.

Book One proves it differently: The funniest character can't see what's right in front of him — and we watch him find it anyway.

From the handoff documents, Book Three "What I Carried" has:

Standard book formatting typically runs about 250-300 words per page for fiction (depending on trim size, font, margins, etc.).

Let me calculate:
- 47,900 words ÷ 250 words/page = ~192 pages
- 47,900 words ÷ 300 words/page = ~160 pages
